The drive assembly at the stern was present only as its mounting architecture, the cradles and heat exchangers and magnetic field generators that would eventually enclose the engine itself, which had not been fully designed yet. That was the part ARIA referred to, in its daily construction progress reports, as "drive system integration pending final propulsion specifications," which was engineering language for we haven't decided yet.

But the spine was there. The spine was undeniably there, and it was larger than Maria had expected even though she had been flying construction cargo to this facility for two years and had watched it grow component by component, and the gap between understanding something's scale from numbers and understanding it from sight was a gap that kept surprising her.

She stayed on the platform for eleven minutes, which was longer than the cold justified. Then she went back through the airlock.

* * *

Commander Patricia Novak's office was what a base commander's office looked like when the base commander had been too busy for three years to do anything about the way her office looked: a desk with a surface entirely covered by things that had been set there temporarily, three monitors, a folded lunar suit hanging from a hook that had probably been installed for a different purpose, and a whiteboard on the wall that had not been fully erased in what appeared to be several months. The most recent writing on the whiteboard was a schedule. The previous writing, visible through the new, appeared to be a technical diagram of some kind.

Novak was standing at the whiteboard when Maria came in, not looking at it but thinking at it, which was a distinction Maria had learned to recognize.

"Cargo manifest for February is short by eighteen percent," Novak said, without turning around. She said this the way she said most things: as a fact that needed acknowledging before anything else could be addressed.

"Supply chain issue at Cape Canaveral. The titanium alloy shortage is real. I've been reading the reports."

"The reports say three months. I think six." Novak turned. She was fifty-one, a naval engineer who had transferred to NASA in 2019 and who managed Luna Base with the organizational style of someone who had been responsible for keeping large expensive systems functional in remote environments for most of her professional life. She was not warm, exactly, but she was direct in a way that Maria found preferable to warm. "Your assignment rotation comes up in April. You're eligible for a transfer back to Earth duty."

"I know."

"Are you putting in for it?"

Maria had been thinking about this since December. She had an apartment in Houston she had not slept in for fourteen months. She had a sister in Connecticut and two nephews she'd seen twice since the impact, once in person and once on a screen. She had the accumulated small frictions of two years on a base where the population was 340 people and the social landscape had been mapped and remapped until everyone knew exactly who they were going to have dinner with and why.

"No," she said.

Novak nodded. She had the manner of someone who had expected this answer. "The drive specifications," she said. "You've read the current draft."

"I've read it."

"Your thoughts."

This was the reason Maria had been summoned, not the cargo manifest. The drive specifications were not her area of expertise in any formal sense: she was a pilot and a mission specialist, not a propulsion engineer. But she'd spent two years living with this project, flying its components, reading every briefing and technical summary that crossed her clearance level, developing an intuitive familiarity with the engineering landscape that Novak had been consulting her on for six months in the specific way that commanders consulted people who didn't have credentials but had judgment.

"The current draft gets us to eleven percent of light speed," Maria said. "Which gets us to Proxima Centauri in forty-one years at cruise. Crew would be in cryosleep for the transit."

"And the alternative."

"The alternative adds four years to the construction timeline and gets us to seventeen percent. Twenty-five years transit time. Meaningfully shorter for the crew's biological age at arrival."

"At a cost of four years we may not have."

The asteroid arrived in 2042. ARIA's latest projection put the probability of deflection at 61 percent, down from the 68 that had been the public-facing figure since 2025. The deflection mission was on track. The probability had declined because more precise orbital modeling had tightened the error bars on the trajectory estimate, which had not improved the estimate's optimism. If the deflection failed, the ship needed to be launched and at a safe distance before 2042.

"We have enough margin for four years if nothing else goes wrong," Maria said. "In my assessment."

"Nothing else going wrong is not a category I plan around." Novak sat down at her desk. "ARIA ran the optimistic scenario with the extended drive timeline at my request. Eighty-three percent confidence of launch completion before the impact window. With the current timeline it's ninety-one."

An eight-percentage-point difference on a one-time event.

"What does Hartley say?" Maria asked. Paul Hartley had moved from Senator Grant's staff to the program's technical advisory board six months ago. He was the one reading the propulsion engineering reports and translating them for the political stakeholders.

"Hartley says the decision is above his level. Which is correct." Novak looked at her. "I've sent my recommendation to the scientific board. I recommended the current timeline with the lower performance specification. I'm telling you this because you're going to be the one flying it."

Maria absorbed this.

"You're telling me before the decision is official."

"I'm telling you because you should know what you're being asked to fly and what it can do." Novak said this without apology. "Forty-one years. Proxima Centauri. If the deflection fails and we launch. You go in cryosleep. You wake up at the other end, if the biology works, and you're in your mid-seventies and you're looking at a star that isn't the sun and you've got four thousand people behind you who trusted you to get them there."

Maria looked at the whiteboard with its half-erased technical diagram and the schedule written over it.

"I know what I signed up for," she said.

"No," Novak said. "You signed up for seventeen years of cargo runs and briefings. You're now being told what comes after that." She paused. "I'm not asking you to decide anything today. I'm asking you to understand what you're deciding when the time comes."

Maria nodded. She stood to leave, then stopped.

"Amy Stokes," she said. "She's been passed to Elena Marsh's second round."

"I heard."

"She has two kids. Nineteen and twenty-two now."

"I know." Novak's voice was level. "That's not the selection criterion."

"I know it's not." Maria put her hand on the doorframe. "I was just saying."

* * *

She went back through the corridor to the pilot quarters, passing three technicians she knew by name and two she didn't, nodding to all of them with the practiced ease of someone who lived in a community of three hundred and forty people and had learned the social economy of a place where there was nowhere to go to be alone except the observation platform in the cold.

She sat on her bunk and looked at the ceiling, which was a standard-issue habitat ceiling and did not have anything particular to recommend it as a subject of contemplation.

Forty-one years. Proxima Centauri.

She thought about the observation platform and the spine of the ship against the stars and the robotic arms moving with the patient mechanical certainty of systems that didn't have opinions about what they were building.

She thought about her nephew's birthday cake, the chocolate one, whether her sister had gotten the right one.

She thought about the column of smoke and earth that had been rising from Massachusetts when she'd looked out the window of Flight 847 and understood, in the first clean moment before the professionalism took over, what the world had just become.

Two and a half years ago that had been a shock. It had sharpened, in the months since, into something more like a project. It was still a project. It was also, she was beginning to understand, the whole structure of the rest of her life, and she had known this for some time, and she was just today arriving at the specific version of knowing it that sat differently in the body than all the previous versions.

She lay back on the bunk and closed her eyes for three minutes, which was all she had before her next briefing.

Then she got up and went back to work.

,  END OF CHAPTER 10 ,

---

# Chapter 11: The People They Left

Thomas Rourke | Jamaica Plain, Boston

September 12, 2028 | 6:41 PM EST

The appeals had mostly failed.

Thomas had filed twenty-nine in the spring of 2026, following up on the three he'd submitted in January. Of those thirty-two, eight had been approved for partial restoration of original funding, which meant about a third of the original allocation and an indefinite timeline for the rest. Twelve were still in review, which was bureaucratic language for yes but not yet or no but not yet and was only distinguishable from the other by subsequent events. Twelve had been denied.

He had expected this. He had filed them anyway because filing them had produced something he hadn't entirely anticipated: a paper trail that was now being used, by three different community organizations in the outer ring neighborhoods, as the evidentiary foundation for a federal civil complaint filed in January 2028 through a pro-bono legal team at Northeastern University. The complaint alleged systematic de-prioritization of impact-affected communities in the reconstruction authorization process. It named the Office of Management and Budget and the Department of Housing and Urban Development and four other agencies.

Thomas was not the plaintiff. He was a witness and a source. He had provided documentation and had answered questions under oath in two depositions and had been careful, in both depositions, to answer exactly what was asked and nothing beyond it, because he was not a lawyer and because the lawyers had asked him to be careful and because careful was how he worked.

He had also, in the two years since the OMB re-allocation, quietly helped three other people start their own community information sessions in Roxbury and Dorchester and East Boston. Not officially. Not through any organization. He had given them the structure of the sessions, the methodology, the list of federal processes and their actual mechanisms versus their theoretical ones, and then stepped back, because the sessions worked better when they grew from the community rather than from him, and because he was aware that his profile, such as it was, was not an asset in every context.

Mrs. Petrovitch was back in her apartment. The Lamartine Street building had been cleared in November 2027, twenty-five months after the impact, with a partial funding restoration that had covered the structural work and left the interior finishes incomplete. She had moved back in anyway, had been living with unfinished walls and a kitchen that was functional but unfinished, had told him when he'd stopped by to check the structural work that it was fine, that she'd spent forty years in the building and she wasn't about to let unfinished drywall change that.

He'd said something about the drywall being finished eventually. She'd handed him a cup of tea and said that she hoped so, in the tone of someone who had recalibrated their relationship with eventually.

* * *

Ben Oakes had been a structural engineer before the impact. They'd met at a conference in 2022 and had stayed loosely in contact the way professionals stayed in contact: occasional emails, a few conference-adjacent beers, the kind of relationship that was professional rather than personal but that had a warmth to it.

On October 15, 2025, Ben had been in a meeting at a research facility in Pasadena when the shockwave hit. He had been recruited within the week, not to the Luna Base program but to something adjacent: a project at the Jet Propulsion Laboratory working on the asteroid deflection mission specifically, the hardware and software required to actually change the trajectory of a 340-meter object in space with enough precision to miss the Earth.

Ben had been the one Thomas had texted the afternoon of the impact. The reply had come three days later. Then nothing for eighteen months. Then an email, brief, saying that he was in a position where he couldn't talk about the work specifically but that he was glad Thomas was doing what he was doing.

The current contact had started in April 2028, when Ben had shown up at a structural assessment conference in Chicago that Thomas was presenting at, and had found him after the session and suggested they get dinner.

They'd had dinner. They'd talked about the work, Ben's and Thomas's, in the way that people talked about work when one of them had a clearance that limited what they could say. Thomas had come away from the dinner with the impression that the deflection mission was more complicated than the public-facing briefings suggested, and that Ben had wanted to see him for reasons that went beyond catching up, and that the reason hadn't been fully surfaced at dinner.

Ben called in September.

"I'm going to be in Boston in two weeks," he said. "Can we get coffee?"

"Sure."

"Not at a conference."

Thomas processed this distinction. "Okay."

"I have something I want to tell you. It's not classified. It's just something I think you should know, and I need to say it to someone who will understand it."

They met at a diner in Cambridge on a Saturday morning, a place Ben had suggested, and Thomas arrived first and watched Ben come in from the parking lot with the particular walk of someone who had been carrying something for a long time and was about to set it down.

"The deflection probability," Ben said, after the coffee had arrived and he'd been quiet long enough to make Thomas aware that the thing he was about to say mattered. "The official figure is sixty-one percent."

"That's what ARIA published in the last update."

"The internal figure, as of six weeks ago, is fifty-four percent." Ben held his cup in both hands. He was not looking at Thomas. He was looking at the table. "There's a targeting problem with the kinetic impactors. The asteroid is a rubble pile. You hit it in the wrong place, you get fragmentation instead of deflection. You get a debris field instead of a single object."

Thomas kept very still.

"The public figure is sixty-one," Ben said. "That's the optimistic scenario from the model that assumes the targeting problem is solvable. The internal figure accounts for the current probability of solving the targeting problem before the 2039 launch window."

"Is it solvable?"

"Probably. That's what we're working on." Ben looked up. "The problem is the timeline. We need to run a precursor mission to map the internal structure of the asteroid before we can finalize the targeting. The precursor mission launches in 2031. It gets there in 2033. That's two years of data processing before we have the internal structure map. Which means the final targeting parameters for the kinetic impactors can't be confirmed until 2035 at the earliest."

"Which is four years before the launch window."

"Four years is enough, if the data is what we need it to be." Ben finally drank his coffee. "I'm telling you this because I think you deserve to know. I think the people in your community sessions deserve to know. Not the internal probability figure, that's not mine to share. But the shape of the problem."

Thomas thought about the community sessions. About Dmitri, who had been asking more pointed questions over the past year, whose understanding of the political mechanics of the situation had sharpened considerably. About Nina, whose daughter was two now and who had started bringing her to the sessions because her regular childcare had fallen through and had kept bringing her because she didn't seem to find the sessions less valuable for the presence of a toddler.

"The ship," Thomas said.

"Yes."

"If the probability comes down further."

"The ship becomes more important. Yes." Ben set down his cup. "I'm not saying it's going to. I'm saying the ship is not just a backup for a worst case. It's a backup for a range of cases that's wider than the public messaging implies."

Thomas sat with this for a moment. Outside the diner window, Cambridge went about a Saturday morning: people walking dogs, a couple arguing quietly at a parking meter, a group of students moving with the directionless energy of people who had woken up recently and had not yet committed to a direction. People who did not know what Ben had just told him. People who were continuing to live their lives in the way that people continued to live their lives, which was the only available method.

"What do you need from me?" Thomas asked.

"Nothing," Ben said. "I just needed to tell someone who would hold it right."

Thomas thought about what holding it right meant. He thought about the thirty-two files in the redirected column and the eight partial restorations and the twelve denials and the twelve still pending. He thought about the federal civil complaint and the pro-bono team at Northeastern and Mrs. Petrovitch's unfinished kitchen walls.

He thought about Dmitri, who had started attending local planning board meetings, who had started asking the planning board questions that Thomas recognized as the next level of the questions he'd been asking in the community sessions.

"I'll hold it right," Thomas said.

They finished their coffee. Ben took the train back to wherever he was staying. Thomas drove back to Jamaica Plain and sat in his car outside the building where he kept his office and did not go in for a while.

He thought about the probability estimate. He ran the math in his head the way he ran structural calculations, looking for the load-bearing element, the thing the rest of it hung on. Fifty-four percent optimistic, internal, six weeks old. The precursor mission in 2031. The structure map in 2035.

He thought about nine billion people who were not on the ship.

He got out of the car and went in and opened the thirty-two project files and began reviewing the twelve that were still pending, because that was the work he could do, and doing the work he could do was the only response to information he couldn't act on that was worth anything at all.

At nine o'clock he closed the laptop and walked two blocks to the community center and unlocked it and turned on the lights and set up the chairs for the Thursday session, which was three days away, and then walked back to his car in the September dark.

He had not figured out what to do with what Ben had told him.

He suspected he was going to have to.

,  END OF CHAPTER 11 ,

---

# Chapter 12: Sovereignty

Dr. Marcus Webb | Mars Station Olympus

April 3, 2029 | 11:14 AM Mars Local Solar Time

The message from Earth had taken fourteen minutes to arrive and would take fourteen minutes to reply to, which gave Marcus twenty-eight minutes in which the conversation could not move forward, and which he was using to re-read the message a third time and look out the habitat window at the rust-colored plateau that had been his horizon for four years.

The message was from the Framework's liaison office in Geneva, which reported to Senator Grant's committee, and it was officially a "coordination update regarding Mars Station Olympus's operational and governance status." What it actually was, in Marcus's reading, was a question: are you still with us or not?

The question had been building for two years.

The Mars station had grown. That was the relevant fact that everything else derived from. In 2025, when the Boston impact had severed communications for four days and Marcus had convened the crew for their vote, there had been twelve people on the station and a hydroponics expansion that could support fourteen. There were now thirty-one people, a third hydroponic expansion underway that would support thirty-eight, a water extraction operation that had exceeded all projections, and a construction program that had added four new habitat modules and a workshop that the crew had taken to calling, with an informality that Marcus found both practical and revealing, "the yard."

They were not dependent on Earth supply. They had not been dependent on Earth supply for eight months. The last resupply cargo from Luna had contained components they'd requested but could theoretically have fabricated themselves, and three months from now, when the next fabrication printer came online, they would be able to fabricate them themselves.

Dr. Lena Shaw had done this. That was the accurate assessment and Marcus had been making it consistently in the crew reports he sent to Earth, which had become less frequent in their actual information content as the crew had become more capable and the specific nature of their daily operations had become less dependent on Earth expertise. Lena had taken the hydroponics operation from a survival measure to a genuine food system. She'd trained two crew members who were now running most of it themselves. She thought about the station's food independence the way Marcus thought about its operational independence: as a condition to be engineered into permanence.

The problem with independence was that it was not the plan.

The plan, the plan Marcus had voted for along with the other eleven crew members on October 19, 2025, had been to commit to becoming a permanent self-sustaining settlement. The plan had not specified the governance structure of that settlement. It had not specified whether a permanent self-sustaining settlement on Mars was a facility of the Framework for Coordinated Planetary Defense, an independent entity, or something in between that didn't have a name yet.

The Framework had a view on this. The message from Geneva expressed the view in diplomatic language that Marcus had learned to read as: you are a facility of the Framework, you receive Framework support and guidance, and your "operational independence" is a practical characteristic of a remote facility rather than a legal or governance status.

Mars had a different view. This was not Marcus's view specifically, although he held it. It was the view that had emerged from four years of thirty-one people solving problems on a planet where Earth was fourteen minutes away and the solutions had to be found locally or not found at all. The view was: we have been solving our own problems. We are good at it. We are going to keep doing it. The question of whether we do it as a Framework facility or as something else is a question that deserves a real answer.

The crew had been having this conversation informally for a year. Marcus had been managing the formal version of it with Geneva for six months. The message this morning was Geneva deciding it was time to have the formal version directly.

He hit reply and started typing.

* * *

Lena Shaw came to find him at noon, which she did on most days because they had both developed the habit of eating lunch at the same time in the main habitat common area. It was the kind of habit that developed in small communities where the schedules aligned whether you planned them or not.

"You sent a reply," she said. It wasn't a question. The station's outgoing communications were logged and the crew had access to the logs, which had been a policy decision Marcus had made in year one and that he'd found, consistently, to be worth the transparency cost.

"I sent a reply."

"What did you say?"

He'd said: the crew of Mars Station Olympus is committed to the mission's goals, including the development of human life beyond Earth. We believe that commitment is best served by governance structures that reflect the operational reality of a self-sustaining settlement rather than the assumptions of a dependent facility. We are willing to discuss what that means in practice. We are not willing to accept a framework that treats four years of demonstrated capability as a circumstance to be managed rather than a foundation to build on.

He told Lena the substance of this. She ate her lunch without commenting, which was her way of processing information that she needed to think about before she had an opinion.

"They'll say we're destabilizing the program," she said eventually.

"They might."

"Are we?"

He'd thought about this. The ship needed Mars. Not operationally, not yet, but the long-term plan included Mars as support infrastructure, a waypoint and a second base of human activity that made the interstellar mission less contingent on a single fragile Earth civilization. That was the plan as written by people who had been writing plans on Earth in 2025 with a particular picture of what Mars would be. The picture was outdated.

"We're not destabilizing it," he said. "We're insisting that the program account for what Mars actually is instead of what the 2025 projections assumed it would be."

"Which is what?"

"A civilization. A small one. Four years old. But a civilization."

Lena was quiet for a moment. She looked out the habitat window at the plateau, at the long afternoon shadows the Martian sun was casting across the equipment yard. "Thirty-one people," she said.

"Is where it starts."

She nodded slowly. She had the expression of someone who had been thinking about something for a long time and had just heard the sentence that named it.

"If we push this," she said, "and Geneva pushes back, we could lose the Framework support. The technical collaboration. The communication priority."

"We could."

"We'd survive it."

"Yes." He held her gaze. "We would survive it. The question is whether we want to spend the next thirteen years in that kind of friction with the organization that's trying to save the human species."

"As opposed to what? Spending the next thirteen years being managed as a dependent facility by an organization that's treating us like an inconvenience."

"That's the choice, more or less."

She looked at her lunch. "What does the crew think?"

"I'm going to find out this afternoon." He stood up. "I'm calling a meeting."

* * *

The meeting was in the main common area, all thirty-one crew members on the station, with two joining by video link from the water extraction site three kilometers away. Marcus had not prepared remarks. He'd found, over four years, that the crew responded better to conversations than to presentations, and that the conversations were more useful to him than the presentations anyway because the crew was genuinely intelligent and noticed things he didn't notice.

He described the Geneva message. He described his reply. He described the choice as he saw it: continue negotiating with the Framework from within its structure, or make a formal request for governance recognition as an independent entity.

The room was quiet when he finished.

Dr. Paulo Ribeiro, the station's geologist and the crew member most likely to ask the question everyone else was thinking: "What do we lose if we formalize independence?"

"Communication priority. Technical collaboration terms would need to be renegotiated. Potentially some supply chain support, though we're mostly past needing it."

"What do we gain?"

"Legal recognition that our governance decisions are ours to make. Formal status in the Framework's multi-sovereign structure as a distinct party rather than a facility." He paused. "Voice. In the decisions that are going to be made about what comes next."

A crew member from the back, a systems engineer named Adaeze who had been on the station for eighteen months and who spoke rarely but with the quality of someone who had thought carefully before she spoke: "The ship. If we're independent, what's our relationship to the ship?"

"That's the negotiation," Marcus said. "We want to be part of it. Not as a facility but as a partner."

"They might say no."

"They might." He looked at the room. "This vote is different from the one we took in 2025. In 2025 we were choosing between two operational modes. This vote is about who we are, legally and politically. I want to be clear about that."

The room was quiet again. Thirty-one people who had come to Mars as researchers and specialists and had become, in four years, something else, were making an assessment.

"Call the vote," Lena said.

The vote was twenty-eight to three for formal independence.

Marcus sent the message to Geneva that afternoon. It took fourteen minutes to travel. The reply would take fourteen more.

He went to stand at the habitat window and look at the plateau and think about what he'd just done. The sun was getting low, the Martian afternoon moving toward its long orange sunset. The equipment yard was quiet. Somewhere out there, in the cold dark between worlds, a 340-meter object was following its mathematics.

He'd just told the people trying to stop it that Mars was going to be a partner in the effort, not a facility.

He thought this was the right call. He also thought the next thirteen years were going to be considerably more complicated than the previous four.

He went back to work.

,  END OF CHAPTER 12 ,

---

# Chapter 13: The Public Number

Thomas Rourke | Jamaica Plain, Boston

November 14, 2029 | 6:22 AM EST

The ARIA briefing dropped on a Tuesday morning and Thomas read it on his phone before he got out of bed, which was a habit he'd developed over four years of watching the numbers, and which he was occasionally aware was not a healthy habit.

The headline figure was 54 percent.

He read the figure three times. Then he sat up and read the full briefing, which was two pages and had the measured, careful language of an organization that had spent a week deciding how to release information it would have preferred to keep internal. The précis: the latest orbital modeling, incorporating new data from the preliminary observation campaign launched in 2028, had revised the deflection probability downward from the previously published 61 percent to 54 percent. The revision reflected tighter constraints on the asteroid's internal structure, which had complicated the targeting models for the kinetic impactors. The precursor mission, scheduled for launch in 2031, would provide the structural data required to finalize targeting parameters. ARIA's assessment was that the probability would improve once that data was available. The 54 percent figure was an honest accounting of current knowledge, not a final assessment.

Thomas sat with this for a while.

He thought about Ben Oakes, in the diner in Cambridge fourteen months ago. The internal figure is 54 percent. That's what Ben had told him, which meant the public number and the internal number were now the same number, which meant the information Ben had given him at professional and possibly legal risk was now on the ARIA website, and Thomas had been carrying it for over a year without knowing what to do with it, and now the carrying was over.

He got dressed. He made coffee. He stood at his kitchen window and looked at the street below, the Jamaica Plain morning assembling itself the way it always did: a woman walking a dog in the cold, a delivery truck blocking the crosswalk while the driver ran a package to a door, the Dunkin across the intersection already with a line six people deep. Life proceeding, the way it always did, the way it would keep doing until it didn't.

The civil complaint had settled in September.

Not fully. Three of the six agencies had reached a partial settlement: a commitment to revised authorization timelines and an independent monitoring office, which the pro-bono team at Northeastern had argued was a structural fix rather than a dollar-figure remedy. Thomas had reviewed the settlement terms and found them honestly modest, which was the accurate characterization of what three years of federal litigation produced when you were arguing process failure rather than harm. The monitoring office was real. The timeline revisions were real. The two agencies that hadn't settled were still in discovery, which would take another eighteen months at minimum, and the two agencies that had denied the complaint outright were on appeal.

Dmitri had called it a partial win. Thomas had called it a record and moved on, which was what you did when the record was as good as you were going to get and the work in front of you hadn't changed.

The Thursday session had grown. Forty-two regular attendees now, up from the original eleven, with a waiting list that Thomas maintained with the same spreadsheet discipline he applied to the appeals files. The sessions had evolved: less introduction, more working meeting. Dmitri ran a subgroup that focused on the city planning interface. Nina had started a parallel session specifically for parents with children under twelve, which met on Tuesday evenings and which Thomas attended once a month as a resource rather than a facilitator.

Thomas poured his coffee and sat at his kitchen table and opened his laptop.

By mid-morning the ARIA briefing had generated well over a thousand news articles. He looked at a sample. The range was what he'd expected: measured scientific reporting from the serious outlets, crisis-register headlines from the ones that understood their traffic model, and the specific flavor of political response that emerged whenever a federal agency published information that complicated someone's preferred narrative. Senator Grant's office had released a statement within forty minutes of the briefing, which was fast enough that the statement had been prepared in advance, which meant Grant's office had known this was coming. The statement was careful. It acknowledged the revised figure, it emphasized the precursor mission, and it used the phrase "honest and transparent assessment" three times in six paragraphs, which was the rhetorical equivalent of a doctor telling you that a diagnosis is preliminary before explaining what it might mean.

Thomas noted the statement and moved on.

His phone rang at 9:03. It was Ben Oakes.

"You saw it," Ben said.

"I saw it."

"I wanted to call before you saw it." A pause. "I should have called last night. I knew it was dropping this morning."

"You gave me the number fourteen months ago," Thomas said. "You didn't owe me a preview."

"I wanted you to hear it from me first."

Thomas looked at the window. The delivery truck had gone. The dog-walker was back, different dog. "The precursor mission. The structural data. You said if the data is what we need it to be, four years is enough."

"That's still true."

"And if it's not what we need it to be?"

Ben was quiet for a moment. "Then we remodel the approach. There are contingencies. This isn't binary."

"What's the contingency?"

"I can't talk about it specifically."

Thomas had learned, over three years of knowing Ben Oakes in this particular way, that what Ben couldn't talk about specifically was usually the thing that mattered most. He'd learned not to push on it, because Ben told him what he could when he could, and pushing on the gaps produced silence rather than information.

"The ship," Thomas said.

"The ship is on track. Luna Base construction milestone last month. The spine is closed. They're starting the habitation ring installation."

"I know. I've been following the briefings."

"The ship is real, Thomas. That's not in question."

Thomas thought about what was in question. Four thousand people out of nine billion. The math that he'd been carrying since the meeting in Cambridge and that he was still, three years later, carrying in the same way: too heavy to set down, too abstract to do anything with directly.

"The community sessions," Ben said. "How are they going?"

"Forty-two people Thursday. The civil complaint settled in September, partial terms."

"I saw that. The monitoring office is something."

"It's something." Thomas turned a pen over in his hand. "Ben, what does 54 percent mean in concrete terms. Not the institutional language. What does it mean."

A longer pause. "It means slightly worse than a coin flip, with real work ahead that could move it either direction. The targeting problem is solvable. The team working on it has made real progress. The precursor data is what we need to lock it down."

"And if the precursor data doesn't help."

"It will help. The question is whether it helps enough."

Thomas sat with this. Enough was a word that was doing a lot of work.

"Okay," he said.

"Okay?"

"Thank you for calling."

There was a pause, and Thomas understood that Ben had expected more from this conversation and was adjusting to not getting it. "How are you holding it?" Ben asked. The same language he'd used in the diner.

"The same way," Thomas said. "Right."

He hung up and sat at the kitchen table for a while with his coffee going cold. Then he opened a new spreadsheet and started a list.

* * *

The Thursday session that week was different.

Thomas had been facilitating community sessions for four years and he'd learned to read the room the way he'd learned to read structural assessments: by the quality of the silence before anything was said, by the body language of the people who came in early versus the people who waited in the hall until the last minute, by the specific way attention organized itself when something in the broader context had shifted.

Twenty-six of the forty-two were there. The rest had sent messages: some were watching the news, some were with family, some were doing what people did when a number got published that they'd been trying not to think about. The sixteen who'd stayed away weren't disengaged. They were processing.

Dmitri was in his usual chair, second row left. Nina was in the back with her daughter, who was four now and had developed the ability to color through an adult meeting without making noise, which was a skill Thomas genuinely admired. Three new faces sat near the door, which was where new people always sat, close enough to leave if they needed to.

Thomas didn't open with the ARIA briefing. He opened with what he always opened with: the work in front of them. The two agencies still in discovery. The appeals on the denials. The monitoring office implementation timeline, which he'd been tracking with the relevant staff contacts and which was three months behind its own published schedule, which was not surprising and was useful information to have documented.

He got about eight minutes in before a man named Warren in the fourth row raised his hand and said, "Are we going to talk about the briefing this morning?"

Thomas had expected this. "Yes," he said. "After the operational update."

Warren nodded and sat back. The room waited with the specific patience of people who are willing to do the thing in order but who need to know the thing is going to be addressed.

Thomas finished the operational update. Then he put down his notes.

"The ARIA briefing this morning revised the deflection probability to 54 percent," he said. "I want to be direct about what I know and what I don't know."

The room was quiet.

"What I know: 54 percent is the current scientific consensus based on the best available data, which is incomplete because the precursor mission that will provide the key structural data doesn't launch until 2031. What I don't know: whether the data from that mission will improve the probability, hold it where it is, or complicate it further. Those are the honest options and anyone who tells you differently is either reading something I'm not or telling you what they think you need to hear."

A woman in the third row, one of the three new faces, asked: "What does 54 percent mean for us? For the people not on the ship?"

Thomas had been asked versions of this question for four years and had given versions of the same answer. "It means the primary plan, the deflection mission, has a current probability that isn't where any of us would want it to be. The secondary plan, which is the interstellar ship, exists because the people who designed this response understood from the beginning that deflection isn't certain. The two plans are running in parallel. The ship isn't the backup in the sense of a plan you only think about if the primary fails. It's an active program that proceeds regardless of the deflection outcome."

"Four thousand people," said Dmitri. Not a question.

"Four thousand people on the ship. Nine billion on Earth. The deflection mission is what protects the nine billion, and that is the mission that all the political and technical energy is focused on. The ship is the hedge."

"A hedge that saves four thousand," Dmitri said.

"Yes."

The room sat with this. The four-year-old in the back was coloring. The fluorescent light in the corner hummed the way it always hummed.

"So what do we do?" Warren asked.

Thomas had been thinking about this since early that morning. He had a list. "The same thing we've been doing, but with more urgency. The monitoring office has to be held to its implementation timeline. The two agencies in discovery need to know that the public attention cycle generated by this morning's briefing is something we intend to use, not ignore. The appeals have deadlines and those deadlines matter more than they did yesterday."

"And the larger thing," Dmitri said. "The political conversation about the ship. About who's on it."

"That conversation is above what we can directly affect from this room," Thomas said. "It is not above what we can document. The record we've been building for four years is the record that exists when that conversation becomes impossible to avoid."

He believed this. He also knew it was incomplete, that a record was not the same as power, that documentation was a form of witness rather than a form of intervention. He'd been thinking about the gap between those two things since before the civil complaint was filed, and he didn't have a better answer than the one he kept giving, which was: do the thing you can do and trust that the doing accumulates.

After the session, Nina caught up with him in the hallway. Her daughter was asleep against her shoulder.

"The new people," she said. "Warren and the two in the front."

"I noticed."

"They're from a neighborhood group in Roxbury. They've been following the coverage and they want to know how we do what we do."

Thomas looked at the child on her shoulder, the specific dead weight of a sleeping four-year-old. "Tell them to come Thursday. We'll show them the methodology."

Nina nodded. She looked down the hallway, at the exit, at the street through the glass doors. "Do you think it gets better? The probability."

Thomas thought about Ben Oakes. He thought about the specific quality of Ben's voice in the diner, which was the voice of a man telling the truth carefully. "I think there are people working very hard on a very hard problem," he said. "That's as honest as I can be."

She nodded. She shifted her daughter's weight. She went out into the November dark.

Thomas locked up the community center and walked back to his car and sat in it for a while before starting the engine. The street was empty at this hour, the November cold coming through the glass, the city quiet in the particular way it was quiet at eleven on a weeknight.

He thought about the precursor mission. 2031. Data by 2033. Final targeting parameters by 2035. Four years before the launch window.

He thought about a coin flip with nine billion people on it.

He started the car and drove home and opened the new spreadsheet and kept working.

,  END OF CHAPTER 13 ,

---

# Chapter 14: Notification

Dr. Elena Marsh | Crew Selection Office, Houston

March 9, 2030 | 10:47 AM CST

The first notification went to a marine biologist named David Harmon, age thirty-four, who had scored in the ninety-second percentile on the behavioral assessments and whose cryosleep compatibility screening had come back with three metabolic markers flagged red.

Elena drafted the letter herself. She had three templates prepared by the legal team: one for candidates in active screening, one for candidates who had passed medical clearance before the protocol update, and one for candidates who had been provisionally accepted. Harmon was in the third category. The third template was the most careful one. It was also the most inadequate.

She rewrote it.

The letter she sent explained the cryosleep compatibility protocol, the biological markers, the confidence intervals. It explained that incompatibility was a physiological finding rather than a reflection of qualification or merit. It explained the appeals process, which was real, candidates with borderline results had a pathway to retest and to request review by the medical board, and it explained what appeals had produced so far, which was honest information: in the first forty-two notifications, two candidates had successfully appealed based on borderline marker scores. Forty were confirmed incompatible.

It said that the selection team recognized the difficulty of receiving this information, and that counseling resources were available through the Framework's candidate support office.

It did not say: you have scored in the ninety-second percentile and you are one of the best candidates we have reviewed and we cannot put you on the ship because your cellular metabolism will not survive forty years of cold sleep and there is nothing any of us can do about that.

Elena read the letter twice, made one change to the third paragraph, and sent it.

* * *

Kim Aldridge knocked on Elena's office door at eleven. She came in without being asked, which was a privilege she'd earned over three years of working in the same building with the kind of daily proximity that either became easy or broke entirely, and in their case had become easy.

"The Harmon notification went out," Elena said.

Kim sat down in the chair across the desk. She'd been running the cryosleep screening protocol for twelve months and had the specific look of someone who had processed a lot of difficult information and had developed the professional capacity to hold it without showing it while also not pretending it wasn't there. "How many are confirmed at this stage?"

"Forty-three confirmed incompatible in the first cohort. Sixty-one flagged for secondary review. The secondary review protocol runs another six weeks."

"That's above the midpoint of the original estimate."

"It is." Elena had done this math many times. The original estimate had been fourteen to twenty-two percent of the candidate pool. The first cohort was running at nineteen percent confirmed, with secondary review likely to push that to twenty-two or twenty-three. "The pool is large enough to absorb it. We have replacements moving through screening."

"The replacements are from a less screened initial pool."

"They're from the second selection cohort. The screening was applied from the beginning. Their cryosleep compatibility is better known at this stage than the first cohort's was."

Kim nodded. She was turning something over. Elena had learned to wait.

"I want to ask you something," Kim said.

"Go ahead."

"The candidates we're notifying. Harmon is one example. The people in this category, compatibility issue, otherwise excellent candidates. What happens to them?"

"They're removed from the mission pool. The letter explains the appeals process."

"I mean what actually happens to them. If the deflection fails."

Elena looked at the window. Outside, the Houston morning was already bright and beginning to get warm, the particular light of a city that was warm earlier every year. "The same thing that happens to everyone not on the ship."

"Right." Kim looked at her hands. "I think about that a lot. I know we talked about it when we were setting up the protocol, the compatibility issue in context of the alternative. But the abstract version and the specific version are different."

Elena understood this. She'd been living in the space between the abstract and the specific for four years. "We're not sending anyone to their death," she said. "We're telling them that the mission they applied for requires a biological characteristic they don't have. The deflection mission is the response to the threat. The ship is the hedge. We are building the best possible hedge."

"A hedge with twenty-two percent fewer of our best candidates than we selected."

"A hedge with four thousand viable candidates instead of four thousand candidates including some who won't survive the transit." Elena kept her voice level. "The alternative isn't better. A candidate who dies during the cryosleep period doesn't arrive. A ship that launches with eight hundred people who can't survive the journey is not a better hedge."

Kim was quiet for a moment. "I know," she said. "I know the logic. I keep knowing the logic."

"The logic is right."

"That's what makes it hard to stop thinking about." She stood up. "I'll have the secondary review preliminary results by Wednesday."

She left. Elena sat at her desk and looked at the letter queue.

Forty-three sent. Two hundred and twelve pending in the first cohort. She had a system for moving through them: twelve per day, each one reviewed, each one personalized at the third paragraph with whatever detail from the candidate's file made them specific rather than a template. It took an hour each morning. She had been doing it for eleven days and had not found a way to make it easier.

* * *

The candidate who called was not supposed to call.

The notification letters were clear about the contact protocol: responses and questions went to the candidate support office, which had a team and a process. The letter had Elena's office as the sender but it did not have a direct callback number. Somehow, a woman named Patricia Novak had found one.

Elena's assistant forwarded the call on a Friday afternoon with a note saying the woman had identified herself as Patricia Novak, candidate ID in the second round, current assignment as Commander of Luna Base. Elena had not reviewed Novak's file personally, she was in the second cohort, earlier in the process, but the Luna Base Commander's name was not one she needed to look up.

She took the call.

"Commander Novak," Elena said.

"Dr. Marsh." A pause. "I got the notification. I understand the protocol, and I know I'm supposed to use the support office. I'm not calling to appeal. I'm calling because I need thirty seconds of your time to ask one question."

Elena looked at the clock. She had four letters to finish. "Go ahead."

"Does this affect my Luna Base command."

A clean question. Elena had expected something different: a challenge to the screening methodology, a request to review her specific results, an emotional response to a notification she had clearly not expected. Instead, one direct question with no performance in it.

"No," Elena said. "Cryosleep compatibility is a mission crew criterion. Luna Base command is a separate command structure. Your position is not affected by this notification."

A pause on the other end. Elena waited.

"Good," Novak said. "That's what I needed to know." Another beat. "I know what the ship needs. I know what the mission needs. I've been building the thing for three years. I understand why I can't be on it."

Elena said nothing. There was nothing to add to that.

"The work continues," Novak said. "Same as before."

"Same as before," Elena said.

The call ended. Elena sat with the phone for a moment. Patricia Novak had spent three years building a ship she couldn't ride. She had called to ask one question, gotten a clean answer, and was done. The work continues. Elena wrote that in her notes.

She went back to the letter queue.

* * *

The twelve notifications for that day took an hour and twenty minutes. She worked through them in the order they appeared: a structural engineer from Vancouver, a physician from Nairobi, a linguist from Seoul, a former Navy pilot from Norfolk who had scored in the ninety-eighth percentile on every category except one. Each file had a name and a number and a photograph taken at the initial screening, and she looked at each photograph before she finalized each letter. It was not efficient. It was the minimum she owed them.

By the time she finished it was past six and the building was mostly empty. Her assistant had left a note on the door: Sofia called. Call her back when you can.

Elena called Sofia.

"How are you?" Sofia asked.

"Working."

"Still the notifications?"

Elena had told her daughter about the notifications in general terms, the way she'd told her about other aspects of the job: accurately, in the broad shape, without the specific files. "Still."

"How many?"

"We're through the first cohort. I'll finish the primary screening notifications by end of next week. Then secondary review."

Sofia was quiet for a moment. "Mom, can I ask you something."

"You can always ask."

"Do you think the deflection works?"

Elena looked at the window, the dark Houston evening on the other side of it. "I think there are good people working very hard on the targeting problem."

"That's not what I asked."

"I know." Elena put her feet on the desk, the particular informality she allowed herself only when she was alone in the office after hours. "I think fifty-four percent means the same as it always meant: it could go either way, and what we do in the next nine years will determine which way it goes. The deflection team is good. The precursor data will help."

"And if it doesn't help enough."

"Then four thousand people leave."

"And nine billion don't."

Elena had no answer to this that she hadn't given before, and Sofia knew all the answers she had, and they both knew it, so the silence that followed was not empty. It was two people sitting in the reality of a number that neither of them had made and couldn't change.

"Paul proposed," Sofia said.

Elena straightened up. "When?"

"Sunday. Dinner at that place on South Congress." A pause. "I said yes."

Elena sat with this for a moment. Her daughter was engaged. Her daughter was going to marry an environmental engineer named Paul who would spend his life in Austin or wherever the two of them made their life, on a planet that Elena was helping four thousand people leave.

"I'm very happy," Elena said. And meant it entirely, and also felt the particular vertigo of a person who holds two true things that don't fit comfortably together.

"I know," Sofia said. "I can hear it."

They talked for another twenty minutes. Elena asked about the ring, which Sofia described in the exact detail that people described rings to their mothers, and asked about Paul's family, and asked about the timeline they were considering. Normal questions. The questions a mother asked when her daughter called with good news.

After she hung up she sat in the office for a while in the quiet. The letter queue would be there in the morning. Two hundred and twelve notifications. One hundred and sixty-nine to go.

She was building a record. Each letter was a record that the process had been carried out with care. That the people on the wrong side of a biological threshold had been told directly, specifically, with the actual facts and the actual options. That they had not been a rounding error.

She closed the laptop and drove home.

,  END OF CHAPTER 14 ,

---

# Chapter 15: The Ring Closes

Maria Webb | Luna Base, Assembly Framework Observation Post Alpha

January 19, 2031 | 09:11 Luna Standard Time

The last section of Habitation Ring Two came in from the east on the back of a cargo hauler that had spent eleven days in transit from the L2 fabrication platform, and Maria watched it dock from Observation Post Alpha, which was a pressurized bubble of glass and aluminum bolted to the top of the maintenance tower at the base of the framework, and which had been her preferred place to be on mornings like this one.

The section was the size of a city block laid on its side, which was a comparison that didn't quite work because a city block had gravity and weight and permanence and this had none of those things in the conventional sense, but the scale was right. Forty-eight meters of curved aluminum-composite structure with the cooling channels already routed inside the wall and the interior framing in place and the connector flanges polished to the tolerance specs that the engineering team had spent three months arguing over. It came in on cold thrusters, patient and enormous, guided by the automated docking system and a team of six engineers in suits on the framework scaffolding who were there less to assist and more to bear witness, because the system was either going to work or it wasn't and human hands at this stage were observation rather than intervention.

It worked.

The flanges met. The connection indicators on Maria's wrist display cycled from amber to green in the sequence she'd been watching for. Somewhere out there in the vacuum a series of bolts that weighed less than a kilogram each were locking a structure that weighed eighty thousand metric tons into the permanent configuration it would hold for at least forty years and possibly forever, however long forever meant for a ship that was going to leave the solar system.

Habitation Ring Two was closed.

She stayed on the observation post for seventeen minutes after the docking team cleared the area. She had a briefing at ten and work to do before it, and she stayed for seventeen minutes anyway, which was the kind of decision she'd been making more frequently in the last year: choose the view. You could do the work later. The view only happened once.

* * *

The mission parameters had been locked in October.

Not the full mission, not the interstellar mission with its forty-year transit and its cold sleep and its arrival at a star that had only been confirmed as having planets nine years ago. That mission remained in the future in the way large permanent things remained in the future, certain in outline, specific in detail, still too large to hold all at once. What had been locked in October was the other mission: the asteroid deflection mission. The one that would happen first. The one that determined whether the interstellar mission was a hedge or a main event.

The parameters: a crew of six aboard the modification vessel Phoenix, launched in 2039, intercept in 2041, deflection operation over a period of four to six weeks, return transit depending on the operational outcome. Maria was the mission commander and had been officially designated as such for three months, which had been enough time for the designation to feel real rather than provisional.

She had spent those three months learning everything that was known about a rubble-pile asteroid designated 2024 XR19 by the catalog and called "the problem" or "the object" by the people who worked with it daily, because giving it a name made it easier to discuss and harder to think about clearly. The internal structure was the variable. The precursor mission, an unmanned survey vehicle called Compass, was en route and would arrive in 2033, and the structural data from Compass would determine the final targeting protocol for the kinetic impactors she was going to place.

She had read every paper on rubble-pile asteroid dynamics published in the last eighteen years. She had talked to the targeting team at JPL for a combined total of forty hours over three months. She had a very clear picture of what she was being asked to do and a very clear understanding of how many variables in that picture were still blank.

Novak had told her: I'm telling you because you should know what you're being asked to fly and what it can do.

She knew. She kept knowing, the way you kept knowing things that were too large to metabolize all at once.

* * *

The briefing was in the main conference room, which was a room with a long table and a wall-sized display currently showing a real-time rendering of the assembly framework from three angles. The rendering was Maria's doing, or rather the doing of an engineer named Brooks who had built it at her request after she'd gotten tired of the two-dimensional schematics that made the ship look like an engineering drawing rather than a thing that existed.

The thing existed. The rendering showed that clearly.

Six people around the table: Novak, Maria, Dr. Harrison from the technical advisory board who had come up from Earth for the week, and three engineers whose names Maria knew and whose expertise she trusted. The agenda was the habitation ring completion milestone and the timeline revisions that came with it.

"Ring Two closed this morning," Novak said. She said it the same way she said everything that was not a question: as a fact that required acknowledgment before proceeding. "Ring Three integration begins in April. If the fabrication platform stays on schedule, which it has, for the last two quarters, Ring Three closes in November. We enter 2032 with all three habitation rings complete and the drive assembly integration as the remaining major structural work."

"Drive assembly is the long pole," Harrison said. He was the kind of scientist who had stopped being primarily a scientist at some point and had become primarily a person who explained science to decision-makers, and he was very good at it. "The propulsion specifications are locked but the integration tolerances are tighter than anything we've built before. Eighteen months minimum, twenty-four months if there are fit issues."

"There will be fit issues," Maria said.

"There will be fit issues," Novak agreed without inflection. "We're planning for twenty months and hoping for seventeen. That puts final integration completion in mid-2033 at the optimistic end."

"Which leaves six years for outfitting, crew loading, and final systems integration before the impact window," Harrison said.

"Six years and two months," Maria said.

Harrison looked at her. "You've been counting."

"I've been counting since October."

The room absorbed this. Maria had made a decision in October, when the mission designation was confirmed, to stop doing the mental work of not counting. The timeline was what it was. There was margin and the margin was real, and the work to be done within the margin was defined, and knowing exactly how much margin existed was more useful than maintaining a professional detachment from the arithmetic.

Six years and two months to final systems integration. Then crew boarding, which was a three-month operation for four thousand people. Then departure. Then whatever came after departure, which was someone else's problem in the temporal sense and her problem in every other sense.

"The Compass mission," Maria said. "Any updates on transit status."

"On track," Harrison said. "Nominal trajectory. The first signals from the approach phase should reach us in mid-2033."

"Same window as final integration."

"Give or take."

Maria made a note. The asteroid's internal structure and the ship's completion would become known at approximately the same time. She found this either a coincidence or a piece of planning she hadn't been told about, and the operational timeline suggested the latter. Someone had aligned these arrivals deliberately, which meant someone had decided it was better to know the targeting requirements and the ship's readiness status at the same moment rather than sequentially. She agreed with that decision and made a note to find out who had made it.

* * *

After the briefing she went to the pilot simulator for three hours.

The simulator was the closest thing on Luna Base to the interior of the Phoenix, which itself didn't exist yet except as a set of components in various stages of fabrication on Earth and at the L2 platform. What existed was a pressure vessel approximately the right diameter fitted with the command interface design the Phoenix would use, and Maria had been in it for an average of nineteen hours per week for three months.

The simulation today was approach and parking orbit insertion at a rubble-pile object of specified mass and cohesion. She had run the same simulation forty-one times. Each run produced different variables within the specified parameter range, and each run required her to make decisions that would, in a real approach, have consequences for the operation. The simulation was designed to fail in new ways. Today it failed in a way she'd seen before, a navigation error in the parking orbit that produced an uncontrolled drift toward the object's surface, and she managed it the same way she'd managed it the last time it appeared, which was methodically and in the right order, and she got the vehicle back to stable orbit with six minutes of simulation time remaining.

She stayed in the simulator after the run ended. The display showed the post-run analysis, the decision tree rendered as a diagram with her choices marked and the alternatives calculated. She'd made sixteen decisions in the run. Thirteen were rated optimal. Two were rated acceptable. One was flagged for review.

She looked at the flagged decision. She'd seen the flag before on other runs, a choice to prioritize station-keeping over instrument calibration in a specific two-minute window. The simulation's evaluation model considered the other choice optimal. Maria consistently chose the station-keeping. She'd been thinking about why for two months.

The answer she kept arriving at was: because the simulation's model assumes the instrument calibration matters more than the station-keeping in the specified window, and I think the model is wrong, and I think the margin for error on station-keeping in approach proximity is smaller than the model accounts for, and I would rather lose two minutes of calibration data than risk a drift event.

She wrote this down in the notes section of her run log.

It was possible she was wrong. It was possible the simulation's model was right and she was introducing a systematic error into her approach protocol. This was the problem with simulations: they told you what their model preferred, not what reality required. She was going to run a variant tomorrow in which she took the model's preferred choice and see what happened. She'd done this ten times before with this specific decision point and the model's choice had produced worse outcomes six times, better outcomes twice, and equivalent outcomes twice.

She logged the run, powered down the simulator, and went to the cafeteria.

Novak was at a corner table with a plate of food and a stack of reports. Maria took her tray and sat down across from her.

"The simulation flag," Maria said.

Novak didn't look up. "The station-keeping choice."

"You've been reviewing my logs."

"I review all the mission commander logs." She turned a page. "I agree with your choice."

Maria ate. The cafeteria was half full at this hour, the lunch crowd thinning toward the afternoon shift changeover, the low hum of four hundred people's daily routine going about its business. A base that had housed forty people when it was built in 2021 now housed four hundred and eleven, with another ring of habitat modules in planning that would bring it to six hundred and fifty by 2034. The base had grown the way the ship had grown: one section at a time, each one adding mass and permanence, until the thing it was building toward was no longer a concept but a fact.

"The Ring Two closure this morning," Maria said.

"I saw." Novak set down her report. She looked at Maria with the direct assessment that was her default mode of attention. "You were on the observation post."

"Seventeen minutes."

Novak was quiet for a moment. Then: "When I was running submarine operations, I had a commander who told me that every time you watch something become permanent, you should actually watch it. Not check the indicators and move on. Watch it."

"Sound advice."

"He died in 2022. Heart. Never saw any of this." She picked up her fork. "I think about that sometimes."

Maria thought about the ring closing, the flanges meeting, the green indicators in sequence. The thing that had become permanent this morning would still be permanent when Novak was dead and when Maria was dead and when whoever eventually piloted this ship to another star had done so and whatever came after that had come after that. The permanence operated on a scale that was difficult to hold in the mind.

"I'm going to get us to that asteroid," Maria said.

Novak looked at her.

"I know the probability. I know what the precursor data might show. I'm going to get us there and I'm going to place those impactors and we're going to move that rock." She said it the way she said things she'd finished deciding: without emphasis, as information. "I want you to know that."

Novak was quiet for a moment. "I know," she said.

They ate in the cafeteria with three hundred and ninety-eight other people who were building the same thing for the same reason and who were not going to stop.

,  END OF CHAPTER 15 ,

---

# Chapter 16: The Cost of the Word

Dr. Marcus Webb | Mars Station Olympus

February 4, 2031 | 08:30 Mars Local Solar Time

The reply from Geneva had taken nineteen days.

Not fourteen minutes and fourteen more, nineteen days of official silence that Marcus had spent watching the crew notice and not comment on, which was its own kind of comment. He had drafted four potential response scenarios during those nineteen days, organized by the Framework's likely position, and had filed them in a folder on his personal drive that he'd titled, with a dry accuracy that had amused him the first day and stopped amusing him around day twelve, "options."

The reply had arrived in the early morning and Marcus had read it before breakfast and then eaten breakfast and then read it again.

The Framework for Coordinated Planetary Defense and Human Continuity, through its Governance and Coordination Office in Geneva, formally acknowledged the communication from Mars Station Olympus dated April 3, 2029 and the governance resolution passed by the station crew. The Framework noted that the resolution raised governance and jurisdictional questions of significant complexity which required careful consideration. The Framework did not, at this time, recognize the independence claim as constituting a legal change in the station's status under the Framework governance structure. The Framework proposed a working group process to address the questions raised, with a formal charter to be negotiated over the following sixty days.

Marcus read the key line a third time: did not, at this time, recognize the independence claim.

Not a rejection. Not a recognition. A working group.

He had been in enough institutional negotiations to understand that a working group was what organizations proposed when they were not prepared to say yes and not willing to say no. It was a structure for managing the conversation while the conversation did not move forward. It was also, in this case, the best outcome he could have reasonably expected in the first response.

He called a crew meeting.

* * *

"They proposed a working group," Paulo said.

"Yes."

"Which means they're not saying yes."

"It means they're not saying no. There's a difference." Marcus had the message on the display in the common area, the formal language glowing against the Martian morning visible through the habitat window. Thirty-one people in the room, which was everyone. They'd been thirty-one for eight months and would become thirty-three in April when the next crew rotation arrived. "The working group is a process. It means they're willing to have the conversation."

Adaeze, the systems engineer, was in her usual seat. She had the quality Marcus had come to rely on of asking questions from the position of someone who had already thought the answer through. "What do we lose during the working group process?"

"Nothing immediately. Our operational status doesn't change. Communication priority is unaffected. The supply chain is the same."

"And if the working group process ends without recognition."

"Then we're exactly where we were before the vote, except with a clearer record of our position and a documented record of the Framework's response to it." Marcus had thought about this carefully. "The vote didn't change our operational reality. It changed our legal posture. The working group gives us a chance to change that legal posture through a process the Framework controls, which is slower and less clean than a unilateral declaration, but more durable if it succeeds."

"And if it doesn't succeed," Lena said.

Marcus looked at her. She was asking the question she already knew the answer to, the way she sometimes did when she wanted the answer said out loud rather than assumed.

"If the working group fails and Geneva refuses recognition, we have several options, none of which are immediate. We can continue operating as a de facto independent entity without legal recognition, which is what we've been doing in practice for two years. We can escalate through the multi-sovereign structure, which takes eighteen months minimum. Or we can withdraw from the working group and issue a stronger declaration, which would almost certainly result in the Framework suspending coordination services."

"Communication priority," Adaeze said.

"Communication priority. Technical collaboration agreements. The relay network that gives us low-latency contact with Earth and Luna."

The room was quiet. This was the cost, and everyone in the room knew it was the cost and had known it was the cost since the vote. Saying it aloud in the context of an actual Framework response was different from knowing it abstractly.

"So we join the working group," Paulo said.

"That's my recommendation. I want the crew's input."

Lena looked at the message on the display. "They have twenty-two people on the working group charter negotiation team. It says so in the third paragraph."

"Twenty-two people who have been thinking about this for nineteen days. We have thirty-one."

"Thirty-one who built a civilization out of a survival station," Lena said. The way she said it was not pride, exactly, but the specific quality of a person describing a fact they were still absorbing. "What do we need?"

"A negotiator who is not me," Marcus said. "I'm the commander. I shouldn't be the person across the table from Geneva."

"Who do you have in mind?"

Marcus looked at the room. Four years of small community had made the selection obvious. "Paulo for the operational arguments. Adaeze for the technical architecture. And you for the mission argument, why Mars sovereignty serves the long-term goals of human expansion better than administrative dependency."

"You want me to argue that to Geneva," Lena said.

"I want you to argue it to the working group. Geneva will be listening." He paused. "You argued it to me three years ago and I wasn't convinced. You're better at it now."

This produced a small laugh from the room, which was the right response to the moment. The tension that had been building since the message arrived shifted slightly, not resolved but redistributed.

* * *

The charter negotiation took eighty-nine days, which was twenty-nine days past the sixty-day target Geneva had proposed.

The delays were mostly procedural: the Framework's legal team had questions about jurisdictional precedent that their own governance documents didn't fully answer, because the documents had been written in 2025 for a solar system that contained one human outpost beyond Earth, and the solar system now contained two, and the second one had just asked a question the first set of documents had no clean response to.

Marcus tracked the negotiations from Olympus, reading the daily summaries Paulo transmitted, adding comments and corrections, watching the Framework's position shift in small increments through the technical language that these things were always conducted in. The Framework's working group had started by asserting that Mars Station Olympus was a Framework facility in full, with the crew vote constituting an operational decision rather than a governance claim. By day sixty it had moved to acknowledging that the station had "operational characteristics consistent with significant autonomy" while maintaining that legal recognition required a formal amendment to the Framework charter. By day eighty-nine it was willing to discuss what a formal amendment would look like.

This was progress. Marcus wrote "progress" in his personal log on day eighty-nine without additional comment, which was his version of satisfaction.

The working group was formally constituted in June. Three people from Olympus, four from Geneva, two observers from Luna Base, and one observer from the Brazilian delegation that had been the loudest voice in the Framework's multi-sovereign structure about the governance questions Mars raised.

Lena's argument, which she had spent two months refining, was built around a single premise: that the goals of the Framework for Coordinated Planetary Defense, which were to ensure the survival of human civilization across multiple planetary locations, were better served by a Mars that operated as a genuine partner rather than a managed dependency, because genuine partners developed the resilience and capability that survival required, while managed dependencies developed only what their managers authorized. A dependency's ceiling was its manager's vision. A partner's ceiling was its own.

Marcus read the transcript of her first working group session three times. She had made the argument better than he would have made it. He sent her a message that said: Good work. She replied: You could have said that three years ago. He wrote back: I wasn't convinced yet.

* * *

The negotiation was still ongoing at the end of 2031, which was the outcome Marcus had been planning for when he'd told the crew this might take years. Geneva was not obstructing, exactly. It was doing what institutional organizations did when confronted with a question they hadn't been designed to answer: it was moving carefully, consulting its member-state representatives, ensuring that any precedent set for Mars would be a precedent it could live with when the next governance question arrived from the next place that needed answering.

Marcus understood this. He didn't like it but he understood it.

What he was less prepared for was the crew.

Not dissent, there was no dissent. The crew had voted twenty-eight to three and the three who had voted against had not revisited the question publicly. What there was, eight months into a working group that met twice monthly and produced careful incremental language, was something more diffuse: a tiredness with the abstract, a hunger for resolution, a growing restlessness in a community that had made a clear decision and was now living in the administrative fog of that decision's consequences.

He recognized the feeling. It was the feeling of people who had been decisive waiting for institutions to catch up. The institutions would catch up, or they wouldn't, and either way the work on Mars was proceeding and the community was proceeding and the station was proceeding, and the legal question was genuine but it was not the thing that would determine whether Olympus survived.

What would determine whether Olympus survived was the same thing it had always been: the work, done well, repeated.
